From ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com  Thu Nov  1 14:24:46 2007
From: ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com (Carlos Konstanski)
Date: Thu, 1 Nov 2007 15:24:46 -0600 (MDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Emacs Question
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0711011337000.4630@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0711011337000.4630@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Thu, 1 Nov 2007, Rich Shepard wrote:

> Date: Thu, 1 Nov 2007 13:42:31 -0700 (PDT)
> From: Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com>
> To: plug at pdxlinux.org
> Subject: [PLUG] Emacs Question
>
>   I cannot find the answer in my ORA book; probably because I don't know
> where to look in the index.
>
>   Now and then I untinkingly, and mistakenly, hit some key combination that
> changes the buffer status bar at the lower left corner from --:-- to -1>:--
> or -1:--. When the former appears, single and double quotes are underlined
> and characters have accents. I can change from the second to third form by
> pressing the Winduhs key with the quote key.
>
>   What do the -1 and -1> signify? What might I have done to produce them,
> and how do I change the display and behavior back to normal?
>
> Rich

>From http://www.gnu.org/software/emacs/manual/html_node/emacs/Mode-Line.html#Mode-Line

-cs:ch-fr  buf      pos line   (major minor)------

cs states the coding system used for the file you are editing. A dash
indicates the default state of affairs: no code conversion, except for
end-of-line translation if the file contents call for that. `=' means
no conversion whatsoever. Nontrivial code conversions are represented
by various letters-for example, `1' refers to ISO Latin-1. See Coding
Systems, for more information.

I use german-postfix input method quite a lot, but I never paid
attention to the mode line.  But I can tell you that I must hit C-\
(toggle input method) to enter the mode.  That's awfully close to
C-ENTER or C-BACKSPACE.

Carlos Konstanski

On Fri, 2 Nov 2007, kurt braget wrote:

> I think it's cuz you're not using vi.

   In 1979 I wrote my dissertation using TDP (Text and Document Processor) on
a HP-3000 minicomputer. This was (is?) a line-oriented editor with a short
line length that required users to end each line with the [Enter] key or the
excess was truncated. To add text, one positioned the cursor at the desired
place and pressed the 'a' key. To modify text, one used the 'm' key; to
insert, 'i' key and so on. It was an extreme PITA, but better than an
electric typewriter, especially when committee mandated changes had to be
made. Every time I have to use vi I'm reminded of this primative editing
tool.

   The output device was also different from what we're used to using today.
It was an IBM Diablo electronic printer; essentially an electric typewriter
fed by the TDP system. One had to stand there and roll in a new page by hand
-- just as with a regular typewriter -- before pressing the [Enter] key to
print that one page. Of course, it was fun watching it print in both
directions, but the amusement faded quickly with a multi-hundred page
document that needed multiple copies.

   So, you stick with vi and I'll stick with emacs.

Rich

Shortly before leaving Prime Computer I started to learn emacs. I was
really starting to get into it, too. But then I went to Apollo and was
bitten by the GUI bug. While I can use vi on occasion, I often have to
get out the cheat sheet to be reminded what key to hit. As for emacs, I
did try to get into it a bit in the last 5 years, but find GUI editors
like Kate for plain text editing, and Word Perfect for fancy documents
to be more to my liking.

So, how do I get emacs to read a vim modeline?  I see that vim has a 
plugin for reading the emacs File "Local Variables" section, but I 
would rather things work the other way around because the vim modeline 
is (like most of vim) nice and terse.

  # vim:ts=2:sw=2:et:sta

vs

  # Local Variables:
  # indent-tabs-mode:nil
  # ...
  # End:

This is particularly necessary in open source projects given that emacs 
defaults to mix tabs and spaces (huh?).

Apparently the '-*-' line must be the first or second line, so for this 
stuff to live at the end of the file it has to be the extremely 
verbose "Local Variables" chunk?

Man, using a computer in 1979 must've sucked, I wasn't even born then. I
didn't use a computer until 2003, and Linux until about 6 months ago. I have
to admit that I use vi for two reasons: when I first saw a team of
administrators at my work using it, it looked cool. I was using microsoft
word before, now I would rather chisel in stone than use that. the other
reason: I've read it's on more *nix machine by default, and that seems
pretty good if you're in trouble, or in a strange environment. I would
probably only get into Emacs just to see whether or not the learning curve
is shaped in a sprial, like I've seen online, I doubt it.

On Fri, 2 Nov 2007, kurt braget wrote:

> Man, using a computer in 1979 must've sucked,

   It was better than the Unisys I used in 1962, and the IBM S/360s I used at
the U of Illinois 1972-1976. Many of us remember the joys of hours sitting
at the 029 key punch machine creating a deck of hundreds of 80-column
Hollarith cards. Then dropping them on the way to the throne room window,
and having hours of fun putting them back in order. The felt-tip pen was the
greatest programming tool: make diagonal lines on the top of the deck and
getting the cards back in order was _much_ simpler and faster.

   Then there was paper tape on the DEC VAXen ...

> I wasn't even born then. I didn't use a computer until 2003, and Linux
> until about 6 months ago. I have to admit that I use vi for two reasons:
> when I first saw a team of administrators at my work using it, it looked
> cool. I was using microsoft word before, now I would rather chisel in
> stone than use that. the other reason: I've read it's on more *nix machine
> by default, and that seems pretty good if you're in trouble, or in a
> strange environment. I would probably only get into Emacs just to see
> whether or not the learning curve is shaped in a sprial, like I've seen
> online, I doubt it.

   Many SysAdmins like vi for config files and other small editing jobs.
Those who know it well use it for longer programs, too. Like most things in
the *nix world, there are different philosophies of how to accomplish a
given task. Ergo, dozens of scripting languages, compiled programming
languages, text editors, etc. Of course, there are also a lot of egos
involved, but that can be found everywhere. :-)

On Nov 2, 2007, at 6:21 AM, Carlos Konstanski wrote:

> Go easy on the kids, Rich.  The emacs learning curve isn't for
> everybody.  Either you have the emacs gene, or you don't.  Anyone can
> learn vi.

*cough*

<pedantic>
Surely, you must be joking. No scriptable syntax highlighting, no  
command history, no filename completions, no mouse support, no visual  
mode, only one buffer to work in, no X version, no diffing, no ftp/ 
ssh sessions, everything based around editing lines, etc. etc. etc.?

Okay, maybe *vim*, I could *maybe* see people learning that, but  
actual vi?
</pedantic>

;)

For those not following the joke, vim, which most current linux  
distros alias the 'vi' command to, isn't actually vi. It's a much  
changed version, because vi was *horrid*.

'vi' was originally a hack to 'ex', which was a hack to 'ed', and  
understanding this lineage (a single line editor, then showing  
multiple lines on a screen at the same time, then adding line  
navigation commands and multiple line "batch" editing features) is  
helpful when explaining to confused folks why incredibly simple  
ideas, like backspace, absolutely fail to work in vi, when the same  
feature works in the same way on pretty much every modern editor in  
the world. (example follows later)

Heck, the whole reason I switched to emacs when I did, was that in  
comparison, using vi (back in the early 90's) took me three to four  
times as many keystrokes to accomplish the exact same tasks as in  
emacs. Sure, I could *sound* really busy, what with all that clacking  
noise (keyboards *really* clacked loud back then, it was a post- 
typewriter thing), but vi was just a dead-end, and it wasn't until  
nvi and vim came along that the vi family was worth even getting  
*near* again...

So, here's my ultimate pet-peeve about vi, if I have a file with the  
following text:
lazy
dog
...with my cursor under the 'd' character, and I was in edit mode,  
and wanted it to read as
lazy dog
..in any decent editor/terminal (as I would define it, of course)  
combo I would hit
<delete><space>.
Two keystrokes.

The ancient and venerable vi would have none of that, it's a  
glorified single line editor, for a world without arrow keys! (Or,  
rather, arrow keys on HJKL, so your fingers are close to them, and  
the escape key directly to the left of the Q, so switching modes is  
easy...... Because designing a universal unix text editor around the  
horribly obsoleted ADM3A terminal keyboard is a *GREAT* idea.)

At least, however, vi had options (much like perl, vi provides near- 
infinite ways of shooting yourself in the foot). Here's some examples  
of them:
<ESC><k><$><shift-J>  (uhm, yeah... that's easy to remember)
<ESC><RETURN><-><$><shift-J>  (natch)
<ESC><shift-{><$><shift-J>  (are your eyeballs bleeding yet?)

Sadly, because I know my vi skills date back to the System5R4 days, I  
assume much has changed, so I wouldn't be surprised if a vi  
descendant (such as vim) now has a macro of some kind to perform  
"jump-to-prior-line-and-remove-any-end-of-line-characters-and append- 
line-from-below-to-current-line-and-delete-the-lower-line", or even  
if vi *always* had some arcane combo that did the same thing, but  
that's not my point.

My point is that vi cannot <delete><space>, because it is, at its  
heart, a *line* editor, and not a *file* editor.

-Bop

On Nov 2, 2007, at 5:00 PM, Rich Shepard wrote:
> Then there was paper tape on the DEC VAXen ...

I was *editing* and *debugging* paper tape as late as 1995, with a  
razor and splices. Really.

Sometimes I wish more people new to the field of computer science had  
an education of what it was like to work with 8-bit systems, 10Mb  
hard drives (if any!), 4Mhz clocks, etc. They think nothing of using  
the money and electricity required to power a small african  
village.... to render and display their latest blog rant.

I'm working on a whole post-2K-era rant about it, modeled after Ed's  
original 1983 rant (disclosure, at *least* two PLUG'ers know Ed),  
here's the original:
<http://www.google.com/search?q=Real+Programmers+Don%27t+Use+Pascal>

I'm trying to make mine a little less angry before publishing, as I  
am wont to venting. A sample:

----
Real programmers actually know their history.

They know that XML is basically a 40-year-old-technology, and that it  
sucks bytes like mad in order to standardize tree structure.. trunk/n- 
branch/leaf. That's about it. After that, XML, AJAX, XHTML, and all  
of the other "X" words aren't programming, they're marketing. They  
know that parsing a 500Mb ?X? static data file is a waste of active  
RAM, and that RAM is a place for user-interactive jobs, not batch or  
background jobs.

Real programmers aren?t afraid of multiple languages, ways of storing  
data, ways of parsing data. In one workflow. A Real Programmer isn?t  
bothered by a technology stack that mixes PHP, Perl, VB, COBOL,  
PASCAL, ASP, C, C#, CFM, and Python for operations, LDAP stores,  
MySQL, Oracle, PostgreSQL, and BDB for data storage, with a Haskell  
operation at the end, and occasional ASM occuring inline.

As long as each thing used is ?used right?, it is properly used.
----


-Bop

> Okay, maybe *vim*, I could *maybe* see people learning that, but
> actual vi?
> </pedantic>
>
> ;)
>
> For those not following the joke, vim, which most current linux
> distros alias the 'vi' command to, isn't actually vi. It's a much
> changed version, because vi was *horrid*.
>
> 'vi' was originally a hack to 'ex', which was a hack to 'ed', and
> understanding this lineage (a single line editor, then showing
> multiple lines on a screen at the same time, then adding line
> navigation commands and multiple line "batch" editing features) is
> helpful when explaining to confused folks why incredibly simple
> ideas, like backspace, absolutely fail to work in vi, when the same
> feature works in the same way on pretty much every modern editor in
> the world. (example follows later)
>
> Heck, the whole reason I switched to emacs when I did, was that in
> comparison, using vi (back in the early 90's) took me three to four
> times as many keystrokes to accomplish the exact same tasks as in
> emacs. Sure, I could *sound* really busy, what with all that clacking
> noise (keyboards *really* clacked loud back then, it was a post-
> typewriter thing), but vi was just a dead-end, and it wasn't until
> nvi and vim came along that the vi family was worth even getting
> *near* again...
>
> So, here's my ultimate pet-peeve about vi, if I have a file with the
> following text:
> lazy
> dog
> ...with my cursor under the 'd' character, and I was in edit mode,
> and wanted it to read as
> lazy dog
> ..in any decent editor/terminal (as I would define it, of course)
> combo I would hit
> <delete><space>.
> Two keystrokes.
>
> The ancient and venerable vi would have none of that, it's a
> glorified single line editor, for a world without arrow keys! (Or,
> rather, arrow keys on HJKL, so your fingers are close to them, and
> the escape key directly to the left of the Q, so switching modes is
> easy...... Because designing a universal unix text editor around the
> horribly obsoleted ADM3A terminal keyboard is a *GREAT* idea.)
>
> At least, however, vi had options (much like perl, vi provides near-
> infinite ways of shooting yourself in the foot). Here's some examples
> of them:
> <ESC><k><$><shift-J>  (uhm, yeah... that's easy to remember)
> <ESC><RETURN><-><$><shift-J>  (natch)
> <ESC><shift-{><$><shift-J>  (are your eyeballs bleeding yet?)
>
> Sadly, because I know my vi skills date back to the System5R4 days, I
> assume much has changed, so I wouldn't be surprised if a vi
> descendant (such as vim) now has a macro of some kind to perform
> "jump-to-prior-line-and-remove-any-end-of-line-characters-and append-
> line-from-below-to-current-line-and-delete-the-lower-line", or even
> if vi *always* had some arcane combo that did the same thing, but
> that's not my point.
>
> My point is that vi cannot <delete><space>, because it is, at its
> heart, a *line* editor, and not a *file* editor.
>
> -Bop
>
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>

On Nov 3, 2007, at 12:25 AM, kurt braget wrote:
> i don't see why you would'nt just type <delete><space> while in  
> edit mode.

Because in a line editor,  there is *no* newline to delete. The  
newline is above you, not on the line to be edited.

Yes, this makes jaws drop and heads explode.

> or if you're on the 'y' of lazy,

So, you have to navigate *to* the line to change, in order to delete  
that <newline> character at the end of the line......

> i've never used plain old vi, which sounds like was pretty good at  
> just pissing people off.

This is why we argue about it. ;)

It's not an actual debate at this point, so much as cheerful goading,  
in hopes that the opposing team will learn from the "home team's"  
successes and failures, regardless of which team a person is arguing  
for.

>  i'm so glad i joined this
> group, i mean, who else am i going to talk to about vi (or vim) as  
> if it
> were shakespeare?

Did you sever maiden vi newlines?

(With apologies to all who haven't read the play with a bawdy mind,  
Romeo and Juliet is fairly, uhm, over the top).

From qhartman at gmail.com  Sun Nov  4 16:27:54 2007
From: qhartman at gmail.com (Quentin Hartman)
Date: Sun, 4 Nov 2007 16:27:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Mounting SATA Optical Drive
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0711041616530.5634@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0711041616530.5634@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <8975a95f0711041627r6e46ee18uc29be601f65fe21a@mail.gmail.com>

On Nov 4, 2007 4:18 PM, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:

>   I cannot find the device by which the Samsung DVD/CD
> reader/writer/rewriter drive is known to the kernel. It does not respond
> to
> sda-g, sd0-9. On the motherboard, it's connected to the SATA-3 socket.
>
>   Anyone have ideas? If so, please share.
>

What kind of computer / motherboard are you connecting it to? If the SATA
controller has a setting to switch it between "native"and "ATA" mode in the
BIOS, it might need to be flipped to get ATAPI devices like the DVD-RW to
show up. It's also possible that your distro is being "smart" and you can
access the drive through /dev/cdrom . Also a "dmesg | grep -i samsung" might
give you some useful information.

-- 
-Regards-

-Quentin Hartman-

/dev/st0m, /dev/st0, /dev/st0l, and the sound/snd devices.

> Does 'lshal' tell you anything interesting?

   Yes. Apparently HAL isn't home on this Slackware-11.0 box:

[root at salmo ~]# lshal
error: dbus_bus_get: org.freedesktop.DBus.Error.FileNotFound: Failed to
connect to socket /var/run/dbus/system_bus_socket: No such file or director

or, I don't have dbus properly set up.

   Perhaps I should upgrade to Slackware-12.0 before looking for /dev/sr0.

> It would be nice to know which option in "make menuconfig" that

Carlos,

   In my .config it's here:

#
# Please see Documentation/ide.txt for help/info on IDE drives
#
CONFIG_BLK_DEV_IDE_SATA=m
# CONFIG_BLK_DEV_HD_IDE is not set
CONFIG_BLK_DEV_IDEDISK=y
# CONFIG_IDEDISK_MULTI_MODE is not set
CONFIG_BLK_DEV_IDECD=y
# CONFIG_BLK_DEV_IDETAPE is not set
CONFIG_BLK_DEV_IDEFLOPPY=y
CONFIG_BLK_DEV_IDESCSI=m
# CONFIG_IDE_TASK_IOCTL is not set

From johnxj at comcast.net  Tue Nov  6 22:28:44 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Tue, 6 Nov 2007 22:28:44 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] USB reset on Thinkpad
Message-ID: <20071106222844.14f7b41d.johnxj@comcast.net>

I have always used a Logitech external wireless USB mouse with my
laptops. My old Compaq had three USB ports and the receiver could be
hotplugged back and forth into any of them at any time and the mouse
continued to work. My new Thinkpad T61 also has three USB ports, two on
the back right corner and one in the middle of the left side. The mouse
works fine if I boot with the receiver in either of the back right
corner ports, but if I ever pull it out that kills both those ports
forever until I reboot. However, the port on the middle of the left
side always works and can always be hotplugged with any device. 

The Thinkpad has Gutsy amd64: Linux Devil7 2.6.22-14-generic #1 SMP Sun
Oct 14 21:45:15 GMT 2007 x86_64 GNU/Linux. It also has the latest BIOS
from Lenovo. The old computer had Feisty (which won't boot) plus a new
installation of Gutsy amd64. Its ports continue to work as before, so
it's not Gutsy.

Today my new Mini-Dock arrove and I am happy to report that it adds
four more USB ports (all in the back, yay!). All four of the USB ports
on the mini-dock work just fine as far as plug and play is concerned.
However, the two in the lower right corner still die if I disconnect
anything connected to them. This means that when I am away from home I
am stuck with just the one USB port on the left side.

1) Have any Thinkpad users run into this?

2) I tried Google, but cannot find a way to reset the USB ports without
restarting the computer. Is there a command that I could use?

Any other suggestions or observations about the two ports in the back
right corner?

From pm at nellump.net  Wed Nov  7 11:39:23 2007
From: pm at nellump.net (Paul Mullen)
Date: Wed, 7 Nov 2007 11:39:23 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] USB reset on Thinkpad
In-Reply-To: <20071107113111.ef6b32ff.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20071106222844.14f7b41d.johnxj@comcast.net>
	<20071107183129.GA24172@nellump.net>
	<20071107113111.ef6b32ff.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20071107193923.GA27215@nellump.net>

On Wed, Nov 07, 2007 at 11:31:11AM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> I thought of that after I posted the above question. However, I
> don't know which modules they are. When I run lsmod I find the
> following modules that contain "usb":
> 
> hci_usb	21020  3 
> usbhid	32576  0 
> usbcore	161584  6 cdc_acm,hci_usb,usbhid,ehci_hcd,uhci_hcd
> 
> I suppose the command would be rmmod <>, then modprobe <>. But which
> one?

Use "modprobe -r" to unload, and "modprobe" to load.  You'll need to
unload the modules in the reverse order that they were loaded,
otherwise you'll get dependency complaints from modprobe.

The two primary USB modules are "usbcore" and  the "Xhci_hcd" modules.
Start by unloading higher-level modules like "hci_usb" and "usbhid",
then trying the several HCD modules, and finally "usbcore". modprobe
will tell you which modules are holding things up if it can't proceed.

Bear in mind that while this might be an educational experiment, it's
probably not going to be too practical for everyday use. Also, if your
Thinkpad's internal keyboard is a USB device, you'll need to enjoy all
of this through an external (PS/2) keyboard (if the dock provides
one), or via an SSH session.

-- 
Paul

The utility which computes and checks MD5's is called md5sum and in
Debian it is in the package coreutils. You can find this out using
dpkg --search  /usr/bin/md5sum

From johnxj at comcast.net  Fri Nov  9 21:42:53 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Fri, 9 Nov 2007 21:42:53 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Backups <again>
Message-ID: <20071109214253.0401bd10.johnxj@comcast.net>

[rant_mode:on]
What's a desktop user to do? There are many backup utilities for Linux, but almost all of them are designed to back up hundreds of Windows boxes to a central server. I have one laptop that I need to back up. What's a desktop single-computer user to do?

For example, my new laptop has a dual layer DVD. It is working fine. But I can't find any backup utility that recognizes it. I have tried backup2l, backup-manager, cdbackup, cedarbackup2, hubackup, keep, multicd, pybackpack, sbackup and simplebackup. I'm on 64-bit Gutsy, but I could figure out how to compile from source if necessary. I have spent hours looking for a backup utility that will back up 16 GB of ~/ to DVDs, but there is nothing. None of them will even use the DVD as a single-layer DVD. They all crash or hang at some point or another. This is just pathetic.

Back in the days of Feisty I used Kdar, but that is not available in the Gutsy repositories. And back then I used a computer that had a single layer DVD. I can install dar, but its man page is useless. Without a GUI for it I am lost.

My backup requirements are not the same as most Linux users. I create new data that I don't want to lose maybe once a week, sometimes less often. I might be working on a homework project in OOo and need to back it up. Otherwise, the only things I have to back up are a few e-mails, none of which are critical. So a daily or continuous rsync to a network drive is ridiculous overkill. What I'd like to do is create a total mirror of my hard drive once a month or so, and also once a month or so a total backup of my home folder. In between I'd like to be able to do click, click, click and make an incremental backup of ~/ to a DVD drive every once in a while after I have created new data that I don't want to lose. Does anyone have any suggestions? Are there other Linux desktop users out there that have the same needs?
[rant_mode:off]

I suggested a VPN setup workshop night for A.T.  Here's another 
Jax-compatable workshop topic.

One of the perennial problems we face at Advanced Topics meetings is
configuring people's laptops for my 1024x768 projector.  With the
right drivers and X configuration, most laptops will drive it.  But
debugging X is a pain for me - I "just don't get it" when it comes
to all the zillions of options for the radeon driver in my T30
laptop, for example, and I don't understand SUSE and Ubuntu and ???
video setup GUIs.  There are many PLUGgers that know a lot more
about X than I do, and there are many other PLUGgers that would
like to make their laptops more projector friendly.

May I suggest a "laptop and projector" night?  Somebody can bring a
4 or 8-way KVM switch, so we can switch between a few different
laptops without constantly plugging and unplugging cables.  If we
get all the laptops properly configured in the time available, we
can use the remainder of the evening to project rude internet
videos (for scientific testing of video codecs, of course).  

If other people  bring projectors and screens also, we could make
sure our machines work with more than one projector.  Robustness!

This is not an "advanced topic", and there would certainly be a lot
of stupid questions, but many of us would learn something, especially
if there are a few people around that actually understand X.  Perhaps
some X programmers (K.P.?) might be amused to see what the unwashed
masses actually attempt to do with their beautiful windowing system.

Keith

Have you looked at Mondo?  Once you figure out the commandline options,
it'll back everything up to a bootable set of DVDs that can restore your
machine from bare-metal.  It'll also do incremental backups.  I don't
know if it makes good use of dual-layer media, but I've found it very
useful in general.

Admittedly, I haven't tried a full restore, just (I think) file-by-file,
but it's pretty nifty.

From michael at writemoore.net  Sat Nov 10 13:49:27 2007
From: michael at writemoore.net (Michael M. Moore)

Subject: [PLUG] Backups <again>

Message-ID: <1194731367.8324.42.camel@drifter.domain.actdsltmp>


On Fri, 2007-11-09 at 21:42 -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:

> My backup requirements are not the same as most Linux users. I create new data that I don't want to lose maybe once a week, sometimes less often. I might be working on a homework project in OOo and need to back it up. Otherwise, the only things I have to back up are a few e-mails, none of which are critical. So a daily or continuous rsync to a network drive is ridiculous overkill. What I'd like to do is create a total mirror of my hard drive once a month or so, and also once a month or so a total backup of my home folder. In between I'd like to be able to do click, click, click and make an incremental backup of ~/ to a DVD drive every once in a while after I have created new data that I don't want to lose. Does anyone have any suggestions? Are there other Linux desktop users out there that have the same needs?
> [rant_mode:off]


Similar, I guess, but probably different enough that what I do might not
work for you.  I backup once a week to an external USB drive using
rdiff-backup.  My two internal hard drives are 160GB each; my external
USB drive is 320GB.  The math works nicely.  However, I don't really
back up the entire contents of my internal drives, just /home, some
other directories I keep user data on, and /usr/local.  Everything else
I could recreate easily if I need to.  I just back up the stuff I don't
want to have to recreate or re-obtain (in the case of downloads).

I keep it simple.  I don't use cron or any automatic scheduler.  I do it
usually on Sundays, sometimes on Saturdays depending upon the weekend.
The rest of the week my USB hard drive sits unplugged and turned off,
collecting dust.  Backups are the only thing I use it for.  I thought it
was a small price to pay for a little peace of mind.

I like rdiff-backup because its basic usage is very simple, even though
it's a command-line app.  Just:

$ rdiff-backup /home/mcubed /media/disk/backup_home/

(where the first item is what you want to back up, aka the "source"; the
second is where you want the backup to be written, aka the "target.")

It only writes data that has changed since the previous week's backup,
and it keeps the backups incrementally.  The only other maintenance
thing I have to do is remove old backup archives I know I won't need
anymore.  If I haven't gone back to something that I deleted more than
two months ago or to a version of something that had changed more than
two months ago, it's very likely I'll never need to, so I use 2 months
as my cut-off.  Obviously, everybody has different needs on this score.
You can keep whatever you want to keep as far back as you have the
storage space for.

The man page for rdiff-backup is rather long and complicated looking,
but that's because it can do much fancier things than I need it to do.
Like I said, basic usage is dead easy.

That doesn't address your DVD issue at all, but I don't write such
things so that's a problem I haven't had to face myself.

Good luck!


-- 
Michael M. ++ Portland, OR ++ USA

of absolute reality; even larks and katydids are supposed, by some, to
dream." --S. Jackson

On Sat, 10 Nov 2007 09:16:24 -0800
"Ali Corbin" <ali.corbin at gmail.com> dijo:

> On Nov 9, 2007 9:42 PM, John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> wrote:
> ...
> > My backup requirements are not the same as most Linux users. I create new data that I don't want to lose maybe once a week, sometimes less often.
> ___________________
> 
> I have similar needs.
> That's on my old computer.  I just bought a replacement, and am
> seriously considering _not_ moving the backup cartridge to it.  I was
> thinking of buying an external usb drive to use for a backup instead.
> But now I'm wondering if a flash drive might be good enough for my
> needs.

I have a couple USB drives that I could use for backups. The problem is
that there is an obscure bug in the Linux USB protocol that causes
nasty things to happen when transferring very large amounts of data. I
can move individual files of any size, but when I use rsync, cp, tar,
or anything else that is going to move 16 GB of data over the USB port
it locks up the computer about two out of three times. Sometimes it
will lock up within a few minutes after starting the copy, sometimes it
gets almost all the way to the end. Occasionally it even completes the
copy. As I understand it the problem is in the way the USB protocol
works on Linux. Keith told me about it once, but I don't remember
exactly what caused the problem. I also don't know if it might have
been fixed.

Today I experimented with NSsbackup, a fork of Simple Backup that was
created in 2005 and added in the release of Dapper. It has the same
problems that Simple Backup has - it can't find any target folders for
where to put the backup  and there is a Save button to save the
configuration changes you made, but it doesn't work. You have to edit
the config file manually, but that does no good because it ignores half
the settings in its own config file. Plus you can have only one config
file, so you can't create a configuration for your monthly, another for
your weekly, another for an incremental, and so on. You have to edit the
configurations every time. And it can't even do an incremental. And you
can't tell it to limit the size of the archives to < 4 GB so they could
be manually burned to a DVD. (Groisofs can't handle a file greater than
4 GB.) Pretty hopeless.

I think I'd be just as well off to fire up K3B, drag files into its
Burn window until the window is full, and tell it to burn them.

From rsteff at comcast.net  Mon Nov 12 09:40:04 2007
From: rsteff at comcast.net (Richard C. Steffens)
Date: Mon, 12 Nov 2007 09:40:04 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Upgrade Record Keeping
Message-ID: <47388FF4.5060302@comcast.net>

Some weeks ago I ran an upgrade of SuSE 10.1. Several things broke, and
at least one (different) thing was fixed. Bit by bit (no pun intended),
I have worked through fixing things. I suspect that there is a "right"
way to administer my system so that when it comes time to run an upgrade
I'll know in advance what things will break and know what I'll have to
do to fix them. By that I mean things I've installed the old fashioned
way, as opposed to things installed with YaST.

Is there some reading I could do to get a better understanding of how
sys admins keep track of these sorts of things? I'd like to be more
organized about the paper log book I keep, or at least sometimes keep
(which I recognize is part of the problem).

-- 
Regards,

Dick Steffens



From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Nov 12 09:56:12 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Mon, 12 Nov 2007 09:56:12 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Upgrade Record Keeping
In-Reply-To: <47388FF4.5060302@comcast.net>
References: <47388FF4.5060302@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0711120949240.31204@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 12 Nov 2007, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Some weeks ago I ran an upgrade of SuSE 10.1. Several things broke, and at
> least one (different) thing was fixed. Bit by bit (no pun intended), I
> have worked through fixing things. I suspect that there is a "right" way
> to administer my system so that when it comes time to run an upgrade I'll
> know in advance what things will break and know what I'll have to do to
> fix them. By that I mean things I've installed the old fashioned way, as
> opposed to things installed with YaST.

Dick,

   I've no idea how SuSE does things, but each Slackware upgrade includes an
CHANGES file; the latest is CHANGES-AND-HINTS because there's so much to
cover.

   Trying to track everything manually can be a PITA and quickly becomes out
of date. Is there a tool that lets you configure and build from a source
tarball, then make a SuSE package from it? That would be the best way of
keeping the system organized. I've done this with Red Hat and Slackware, so
all installed software (well, 97.31% anyway) are listed in /var/log/packages
and I know both what's installed and which version is the most recent.

   Another suggestion, if you can, is to upgrade a less-critical system
first, find out what's broken there and fix it before upgrading your most
critical system. I do my notebook upgrades first, and that teaches me what I
need to do on the desktop server/workstation.

HTH,

Rich

Applied Ecosystem Services, Inc.        |            Innovation
<http://www.appl-ecosys.com>     Voice: 503-667-4517      Fax: 503-667-8863

Subject: [PLUG] Upgrade Record Keeping

> (which I recognize is part of the problem).

>
> Dick Steffens

Always test on a dev machine before doing stuff on production!  Keep a local 
package repo mirror (like the way gentoo recommends that you use one machine 
in your network as a local portage tree) so that all boxes get the identical 
packages.  I update my server only when I feel that the gentoo portage tree 
is especially stable, and I have installed and tested the updates on at 
least 2 other boxes with the same architecture (my amd64 box doesn't count 
because my server is x86).  My dream setup is an openmosix cluster of 3 
identical machines.  At update time, I would take one node out of the 
cluster and update it, and test it well before updating the rest of the 
nodes.

Carlos Konstanski


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Mon Nov 12 10:58:10 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Mon, 12 Nov 2007 10:58:10 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Upgrade Record Keeping
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0711120949240.31204@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <47388FF4.5060302@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0711120949240.31204@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <200711121058.10320.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Rich Shepard
# on Monday 12 November 2007 09:56:

>Trying to track everything manually can be a PITA and quickly becomes
> out of date. 

Especially if you're installing into /usr/local and then upgrade the 
base system (your bin, lib, man gets masked, "fun" ensues.)

I use stow and recently reduced almost everything in /usr/local strictly 
to symlinks.  I also have this cron job to checkup on it.

  30 04 * * * find /usr/local/{lib,bin,sbin,include,man,share} -type f

> Is there a tool that lets you configure and build from a 
> source tarball, then make a SuSE package from it?

How about checkinstall?  I've had some trouble with that though where 
the hacks to the shared I/O libraries (which checkinstall uses for its 
hooks) don't jive with what the install process is doing -- with the 
nvidia installer in particular.

Perhaps something which builds in a chroot on unionfs would be more 
robust?  Just grab the files from the write layer of the union, right?


I also like to keep track of what the package manager is doing with 
config files, thus I have /etc under svk ala:

 http://www.askbjoernhansen.com/archives/2005/02/23/put_etc_under_s.html

That's actually a bit tricky to setup though (and I don't recommend 
following Ask's instructions exactly.)  I've had better luck with 
creating an empty etc dir in the repository, checkout to /tmp, then 
hack ~/.svk/config, cd etc, `svk propset svn:ignore .` (then ":r! svk 
st" and pare that down.)

Maybe something with git or some other system would be easier to get 
started and manage.  I do like the ability to run 'svk st' and see what 
has been created or changed, and I run this as a cron job every 30 min:

  http://scratchcomputing.com/svn/VCfs/trunk/vc-checkup

And I occasionally dream about getting my dotfiles (~/.foo) under 
control.  Any suggestions there?

Perhaps CFEngine offers some of that functionality?  I haven't heard 
much about how useful it is for a single system.

--Eric
-- 
"Left to themselves, things tend to go from bad to worse."
--Murphy's Corollary
---------------------------------------------------
    http://scratchcomputing.com

From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Mon, 12 Nov 2007 20:35:29 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] NFS for Dummies
Message-ID: <20071112203529.d0c29e3d.johnxj@comcast.net>

I'm trying to get my desktop and laptop to see each other. The laptop
is brand new and has a fresh install of Gutsy amd64. The desktop is
several months old and has a (then) fresh install of Feisty, amd64.
Both have gigabit ethernet, and both are connected through my home
ethernet wiring, which is also all gigabit capable (theoretically).
Ifconfig says the following:

Desktop:	inet addr:192.168.0.101  Bcast:192.168.0.255 Mask:255.255.255.0 
Laptop:	inet addr:192.168.0.102 Bcast:192.168.0.255 Mask:255.255.255.0

Both work fine as far as surfing the net and stuff. Each can ping the
other. But the Feisty and Gutsy GUIs cannot get either one able to
browse or mount the folders on the other. Of course, there is no
documentation for the GUIs. I did previously get it working with my old
computer, that is, the desktop could see the old laptop, although I
never could get the old laptop to be able to mount the desktop share.

I'm sure this would take about three minutes for someone who understood
how networks and permissions work. I've spent about four hours googling
and trying various instructions, but nothing has worked so far.

I recall that getting the desktop to see the old laptop required
adjusting permissions. However I cannot find the GUI where the
permissions were set. If I recall correctly, it was in System >
Administration > SELinux, but that is not there in Gutsy - Ubuntu
switched to AppArmor for Gutsy, and I can't find any documentation to
tell me how to use it. Meantime, I could swear that Feisty was using
SELinux, but that is no longer there either and, according to Synaptic,
it is not installed.

I could use some diagnostic commands that would at least tell me what
is wrong, missing or misconfigured.

It would also help if I knew how to specify an NFS network share from
the command line - that is, the syntax. The share on the desktop
is /media/md3 and the name of the desktop is Devil6. The mount point
for it on the laptop is /media/Devil6_md3. Would it be correct to write
the command:

sudo mount nfs:/Devil6/media/md3 /media/Devil6_md3

Because I tried that and it puked it up. I need to know how to specify
"look for an NFS share." I never know where to use a colon, where to
use slashes, or how many. And a diagnostic command to figure out what
the laptop thinks the desktop share is called would also be helpful.

John,

While I can't say I've got the best solution, I do have NFS working on 
my home system. I am NOT an expert at NFS, I just got it to work for me.

My computer (pc014.kb0odu.us) is a NFS server (running SuSE). In 
/etc/exports I have the following lines:

/etc *(ro,sync)
/mp3 *(rw,sync)
/home/vmware *(rw,sync)
/iso *(rw,sync)

While I have a script to mount / unmount the volumes, this is really 
what it sums up to on pc016.kb0odu.us (my Kubuntu Gutsy AMD64 laptop):

sudo mount -t nfs -O rsize,wsize=8192,timeo=14,intr pc014:/iso 
/iso/pc014 > /dev/null

Hopefully that works for you.

I should put some security around it using PAM, but I just haven't got 
to that yet. But, first I need to upgrade my server from SuSE to Ubuntu.

Tim

From tkubaska at charter.net  Tue Nov 13 13:39:51 2007
From: tkubaska at charter.net (tkubaska at charter.net)
Date: Tue, 13 Nov 2007 13:39:51 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Eclipse is hosting a DemoCamp
Message-ID: <20071113163951.5D4PL.245433.root@fepweb15>

Please let me announce a neat opensource happening in our area.
http://wiki.eclipse.org/Portland_DemoCamp

It happens on Monday 12/10 7PM at McMenamins Kennedy School
http://www.mcmenamins.com/index.php?loc=57

It's free. 

During November and December, Eclipse DemoCamps are happening around the world. Eclipse DemoCamp is an opportunity to showcase all of the cool interesting technology being built by the Eclipse community. It is also an opportunity for you to meet Eclipse enthusiasts in your city. 

The format of the DemoCamp is pretty informal. The idea is for a group of Eclipse enthusiasts to meet up and demo what they are doing with Eclipse. The demos can be of research projects, Eclipse open source projects, applications based on Eclipse, commercial products using Eclipse, whatever you think might be of interest to the attendees. The only stipulation is that it is Eclipse related. 

So please look at the wiki, and if you'd like to attend, sign up. Even better, if you want to give a demo, sign up for that too.

 -ted

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Tue Nov 13 15:07:41 2007
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Tue, 13 Nov 2007 15:07:41 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Problem With Fedora 6,7 and 8
In-Reply-To: <4739BDE0.6070001@newsguy.com>
References: <4739BDE0.6070001@newsguy.com>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0711131507l8d585afs64be94d2709bcda5@mail.gmail.com>

On Nov 13, 2007 7:08 AM, Keith <ac7xclnx at newsguy.com> wrote:
>
> I have a dell dimension e510 computer and when I attempt to install
> Fedora the kernel and init file load and then get a Ready. message,but
> fedora just hangs and will go no further.
> Any ideas on how to get around this problem? I have asked in Fedora chat
> channels and forums but have only been meet with silence. I can install
> the CentOS and Ubuntu on the computer, but I just get a Ready. when I
> try to install Fedora.
>

One workaround I have seen is booting from an USB key and installing
from the DVD (or network).

The Fedora bug is https://bugzilla.redhat.com/show_bug.cgi?id=239585.
It sounds like it is a bug in the interaction between ISOLINUX and the
BIOS.  Here is the workaround mentioned:

It turns out that this isn't a kernel issue at all.  Rather, it is an
interaction between ISOLINUX (specifically, the ISOLINUX menu system) and the
BIOS.  If one holds down the Shift key to bring up the ISOLINUX prompt and
launches the kernel from there, this bug isn't triggered.

 - Ian

Subject: [PLUG] Unlinked web pages with obscure names - mildly secure?
Message-ID: <20071114155824.GA7142@gate.kl-ic.com>


Apache/Web question:

I have a few https web pages on my site with URLs like cj33wq.html .
These drive CGI scripts that do stuff for clients, send me messages,
etc.  They are not linked to (unless my clients do so - bad client!). 
Nothing falls apart if the bad guys find them and start frobbing on
them;  it is just mildly inconvenient.  While most are password
protected, some are not (like the page where they prove who they are
then tell me what they want for a password).

If I understand Apache and web service, there is no way that the bad
guys can find these pages without an exhaustive search (which would
be obvious from the logs) or by listening in on my client's traffic.

Am I mistaken?  Are there simple ways to ask Apache for an index of
all the public but unlinked pages on a website?

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From nickaubert at gmail.com  Wed Nov 14 12:08:15 2007
From: nickaubert at gmail.com (Nick Aubert)
Date: Wed, 14 Nov 2007 12:08:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Unlinked web pages with obscure names - mildly secure?
In-Reply-To: <20071114155824.GA7142@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20071114155824.GA7142@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <55ef240d0711141208x3e340d2cmff372293e4ce37d6@mail.gmail.com>

Apache itself has no way of knowing which pages are linked to.  With a
little shell-fu, you should be able to run some kind of grep command
to check the pages in your web content area.

If you haven't already, you should confirm that apache won't serve up
a directory listing if a browser is pointed to a directory rather than
a file in your web content area.  If apache lets you browse
directories, the Bad Guys could find your pages that way and mildly
inconvenience you.  Turning off directory listing also buys you a
little security through obscurity so it's generally a good thing.

The -Indexes option (with the hyphen in front) should prevent that
from happening.  You can add it like this:

<LocationMatch "^/+$">
Options -Indexes
</LocationMatch>

-Nick

--
KTHNXBYE

From tkubaska at charter.net  Wed Nov 14 23:46:24 2007

About the VPN discussion ...

I typically run ssh with port forwarding to get a GUI on machines in Corvallis and the OSU sysadmins assure me this is secure. The GUI is very responsive.

I can connect from a Linux or a Windows machine to Linux at OSU this way.

Directions how to do this (Linux to Linux) are on our wiki 
http://wiki.eclipse.org/How_to_VNC_to_ecf2
Well, minus the passwords which you cannot have.

increments daily.)

A little snooping with man about zmd pointed me to rug, which may be the
answer to the recommendation for installing from rpms. Here's the first
part of man rug:

rug is the command-line interface to the ZENworks Linux Management (ZLM)
agent. It works with the ZLM daemon to install, update, and remove
software according to the commands you give it. The software which it
installs can be from ZENworks 7 Linux Management, ZENworks 6.6.x Linux
Management servers, aptrpm repositories, as well as local files.

>    Another suggestion, if you can, is to upgrade a less-critical system

> critical system. I do my notebook upgrades first, and that teaches me what I
> need to do on the desktop server/workstation.

From tonyr42 at gmail.com  Thu Nov 15 09:59:34 2007
From: tonyr42 at gmail.com (Tony Rick)
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2007 09:59:34 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Getting a USB device on Mac
In-Reply-To: <d0ad9b7e0711142209o50d817dp28dbf50c0e790e96@mail.gmail.com>
References: <d0ad9b7e0711142209o50d817dp28dbf50c0e790e96@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4da3d49b0711150959wee17cd4kf73690270344c094@mail.gmail.com>

On my (really old) iMac running Ubuntu Feisty
(7.04), a flash drive shows up as /dev/sda1.  After connecting the device to
a USB
port, run the command 'dmesg' on the command line in a terminal.  If the
device
was recognized, the last several lines will report a lot of information
about it,
including what device was assigned.

Not to get too technical, in Ubuntu (Debian? in general?) the USB driver is
implemented 'on top of' the scsi (sd) driver, and so USB devices tend to
show up as
scsi devices (/dev/sda, for example).  USB Keyboard and Mouse are special
exceptions. Since the devices are assigned dynamically (under udev control?
somebody correct me if I'm wrong here), you cannot predict what the device
assignment will be ahead of time, unless you don't use more than one USB
device, or don't ever change the USB devices once attached.

- tony

I think we need to determine if the OP is running Linux on his Mac, or Mac
OS X on his Mac.
>From the question it sounds like Mac OS X.
If it is Linux it should show up on the /dev tree like any other Linux,
depending on the kernel it could be a few different things, such as
/dev/sd[x] or /dev/usb[x].
If Mac OS X, you have to remember to speak BSDish.  When I plug in a USB
flash drive on my work Powerbook, it shows up on /dev/disk1s1.
I don't know about a gps device though since I don't have one.

Good point; I made a weak assumption.

The question is how to identify what device is assigned.  On my iBook it
looks like a flash drive is identified as having a file system and so shows
up as a disk.  I doubt that the gps
device would show up that way.  The GPSD site gpsd.berlios.de says that OS X
is OK, but the usb drivers must
be updated to the most current versions.

I don't see the command *dmesg *on my iBook (10.3.9), but the file
*/var/log/syslog
*has a one line report about the device *disk1s1 *showing up when I plug in
the flash drive.  Maybe
something similar will happen when the gps device is attached.

To see the bottom of */var/log/syslog *in OS X, after attaching
 the gps device to the USB port, open a terminal
(Go->Utilities->Terminal) and type *tail /var/log/syslog* .

- tony

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Thu Nov 15 13:22:25 2007
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason Martin)
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2007 13:22:25 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Unlinked web pages with obscure names - mildly secure?
In-Reply-To: <20071114155824.GA7142@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20071114155824.GA7142@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840711151322q579d211bof97e5915f0c9938c@mail.gmail.com>

On Nov 14, 2007 7:58 AM, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
>
> Apache/Web question:
>
> I have a few https web pages on my site with URLs like cj33wq.html .
> These drive CGI scripts that do stuff for clients, send me messages,
> etc.  They are not linked to (unless my clients do so - bad client!).
> Nothing falls apart if the bad guys find them and start frobbing on
> them;  it is just mildly inconvenient.  While most are password
> protected, some are not (like the page where they prove who they are
> then tell me what they want for a password).
>
> If I understand Apache and web service, there is no way that the bad
> guys can find these pages without an exhaustive search (which would
> be obvious from the logs) or by listening in on my client's traffic.
>
> Am I mistaken?  Are there simple ways to ask Apache for an index of
> all the public but unlinked pages on a website?

Do you have any external links on any of those pages?  Your client's
web browser will happily send the Referer: header to the web server of
any external links.  Additionally, you are relying on your client's
browser being implemented properly to NOT send Referer: if they type
an address in manually or click a bookmark.

Jason


From admin at mindblowingidea.com  Thu Nov 15 13:42:57 2007
From: admin at mindblowingidea.com (Pomeroy Lab)
Date: Thu, 15 Nov 2007 13:42:57 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] New Distro Ultumix. I designed it myslef.
Message-ID: <844713.5981.qm@web509.biz.mail.mud.yahoo.com>

Well in short I started out with PCLinuxOS-2007, added all the codecs and packages most people will ever need, added the legally free Windows interface, added my own boot up picture, added fasterfox for more speed, and several other things. 

I didn't update everything because I found that by doing that some older drivers were replaced and it would not work on older machines. So I left part of it alone. PCLinuxOS came with a program called FrostWire that is only good for downloading porn so I removed it and added Gnomesword and some other goodies. I forgot to add FileZilla before I made it. I also forgot to set OpenOffice.org to save in Microsoft format by default so that no one would cry because Microsoft Office can't open different formats. 

Eventually I found that the following command worked and made the first version.

mklivecd --tmp /home/owner/tmp --debug UltumixLight_v0.0.0.1.iso

It is a Live distrobution so you can try it before you install it. Unfortunitly since it has everything it is a 1.3GB .iso file so it must be put on a DVD. Yes thats right you have to have a DVD burner. But it works out of the box! YAY. 

Why did I do this? Well I don't like Ubuntu and neither do my customers for three reasons. I like PCLinuxOS but it requires that you update it and know how to select and install all of the codecs and it also does not have a completely Windows like interface. This means an extra hour of downloading updates and programs before I'm done with my customer's computer. Ultumix eliminates this problem. 

Enjoy.

Soon to follow: Christian Edition, video game Edition, Sports Lover's Edition, and hopefully 64 bit edition.

/dev/sda4: Attempt to read block from filesystem resulted in short read

fsck.ext3:  Attempt to read block from filesystem resulted in short read

smart-ass remarks.)

I have an HP tx1000 and I can't get the wired internet connection to work.
All other linux boxes in the house work, wired and wireless.

I first installed Feisty, and the way I got the wireless card to work was to
wget the dell driver, worked like a charm. Feisty was great, but the upgrade
was too tempting. I was able to follow a useful thread on how to configure
wireless in the ubuntu forums, only because my wired internet connection was
working, since I had to apt-get install a few things. Since I've upgraded to
Gutsy, the wired connection won't work. I've followed a lot of threads that
say it's because you have to disable ipv6, but I've tried that and it
doesn't work. The main goal is to get my wireless card to work, which there
are plenty of threads on, but they all pre-assume that your wired internet
connection works. I've also seen a lot of posts about this problem with
other machines, and there doesn't seem to be a straight forward answer for
it (yet). So maybe we can double post in the ubuntu forums?

Also, by default, my usb ports aren't recognizing anything, but internet is
more important.

Please let me know what output I need to provide from my machine to help
diagnose, or any insights you might have. Thanks!

I see nothing in /var/log/system.log, strange, maybe the report level
is set to MUTE, joking.

These are all the devices I am seeing:

paul:~ paul$ ls -l /dev/disk*
brw-r-----   1 root  operator   14,   0 Nov  5 14:16 /dev/disk0
brw-r-----   1 root  operator   14,   1 Nov  5 14:16 /dev/disk0s1
brw-r-----   1 root  operator   14,   2 Nov  5 14:16 /dev/disk0s2

Must there be a custom driver for each USB device, or is there some
standard that might be allowing it to "just work"?

On Nov 15, 2007 12:38 PM, Tony Rick <tonyr42 at gmail.com> wrote:
>
> On Nov 15, 2007 11:05 AM, Matt McKenzie <lnxknight at gmail.com> wrote:
>
> > On Nov 15, 2007 9:59 AM, Tony Rick <tonyr42 at gmail.com> wrote:
> >
> > > On my (really old) iMac running Ubuntu Feisty
> > > (7.04), a flash drive shows up as /dev/sda1.  After connecting the
> > device
> > > to
> > > a USB
> > > port, run the command 'dmesg' on the command line in a terminal.  If the
> > > device
> > > was recognized, the last several lines will report a lot of information
> > > about it,
> > > including what device was assigned.
> > >
> > > Not to get too technical, in Ubuntu (Debian? in general?) the USB driver
> > > is
> > > implemented 'on top of' the scsi (sd) driver, and so USB devices tend to
> > > show up as
> > > scsi devices (/dev/sda, for example).  USB Keyboard and Mouse are
> > special
> > > exceptions. Since the devices are assigned dynamically (under udev
> > > control?
> > > somebody correct me if I'm wrong here), you cannot predict what the
> > device
> > > assignment will be ahead of time, unless you don't use more than one USB
> > > device, or don't ever change the USB devices once attached.
> > >
> > >
> > I think we need to determine if the OP is running Linux on his Mac, or Mac
> > OS X on his Mac.
>
>
> Good point; I made a weak assumption.
>
> The question is how to identify what device is assigned.  On my iBook it
> looks like a flash drive is identified as having a file system and so shows
> up as a disk.  I doubt that the gps
> device would show up that way.  The GPSD site gpsd.berlios.de says that OS X
> is OK, but the usb drivers must
> be updated to the most current versions.
>
> I don't see the command *dmesg *on my iBook (10.3.9), but the file
> */var/log/syslog
> *has a one line report about the device *disk1s1 *showing up when I plug in
> the flash drive.  Maybe
> something similar will happen when the gps device is attached.
>
> To see the bottom of */var/log/syslog *in OS X, after attaching
>  the gps device to the USB port, open a terminal
> (Go->Utilities->Terminal) and type *tail /var/log/syslog* .
>
> - tony
>

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Fri Nov 16 09:15:15 2007
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Fri, 16 Nov 2007 09:15:15 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] XY Graph to .gif file
In-Reply-To: <473DBF7F.8030903@frankhunt.com>
References: <473DBF7F.8030903@frankhunt.com>
Message-ID: <473DD023.2070401@seitzassoc.com>

Frank Hunt wrote:
> I need to generate via cron, a simple x,y graph with the output going to 
> an image file, preferably .gif.  I don't want it to use the display 
> since the generated file is to be included in a web page.
> 
> Ideas appreciated . . .
> 
> fh
> 

gnuplot can do this.  Depending on how gnuplot is built, it will 
likely require X11 libraries, but I don't think it will insist on 
opening an X11 display.

galens at toto:~$ cat gnuplot_cmds
set terminal gif
set output 'sin.gif'
plot sin(x)
galens at toto:~$ gnuplot gnuplot_cmds
galens at toto:~$ ll sin.gif
-rw-r--r--  1 galens galens 6871 Nov 16 09:11 sin.gif

galen

	<d0ad9b7e0711160859n1e98ac92t529f106530a09d6a@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840711161054o54da426cj268481954d16a6d4@mail.gmail.com>

On Nov 16, 2007 8:59 AM, Paul Charles Leddy <pcleddy at gmail.com> wrote:
> Ya, this is on MacBook "Pro".
>
> I see nothing in /var/log/system.log, strange, maybe the report level
> is set to MUTE, joking.
>
> These are all the devices I am seeing:
>
> paul:~ paul$ ls -l /dev/disk*
> brw-r-----   1 root  operator   14,   0 Nov  5 14:16 /dev/disk0
> brw-r-----   1 root  operator   14,   1 Nov  5 14:16 /dev/disk0s1
> brw-r-----   1 root  operator   14,   2 Nov  5 14:16 /dev/disk0s2
>
> Must there be a custom driver for each USB device, or is there some
> standard that might be allowing it to "just work"?

OS X has multiple ways to access device, including through the BSD
part of the kernel (/dev/*) or through the I/O registry.

http://developer.apple.com/documentation/DeviceDrivers/Conceptual/AccessingHardware/AH_Other_APIs/chapter_2_section_2.html

If you have Xcode installed there should be a utility called I/O
Registry Explorer you could poke around in to see if something is
registered there.

http://developer.apple.com/documentation/DeviceDrivers/Conceptual/IOKitFundamentals/TheRegistry/chapter_4_section_3.html

If you don't have Xcode installed, you can probably find out some
information from System Profiler.  Unfortunately I don't know much
else beyond that :-)

Jason

Nov 14 00:50:03 kurt-laptop kernel: [    3.880000] forcedeth.c: Reverse

Nov 14 00:50:03 kurt-laptop kernel: [    6.316000] forcedeth: using HIGHDMA
Nov 14 00:50:03 kurt-laptop kernel: [    6.836000] eth0: forcedeth.c:
subsystem: 0103c:30bf bound to 0000:00:14.0
Nov 14 00:50:04 kurt-laptop kernel: [   23.056000] eth0: no link during

From: bill at billbarry.org (Bill Barry)

Subject: [PLUG] Gutsy upgrade on HP tx1000 internet issue

	<422ec1510711161655j782e4a6dkfa193d66bbfd5ae9@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <af070b010711162122k3f47459ar9173f80805f1df83@mail.gmail.com>

On Nov 16, 2007 4:55 PM, kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> wrote:

> Nov 14 01:01:03 kurt-laptop dhcdbd: message_handler: message handler not
> found under /com/redhat/dhcp/eth0 for sub-path eth0.dbus.get.reason
>

This could be the problem. It has been reported as a bug, but I don't see
that it is resolved.
https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/dhcdbd/+bug/93360

As suggested  you could uninstall dhcdbd and set up eth0 as a static
network.

In /etc/network/interfaces replace
iface eth0 inet dhcp
with
auto eth0
iface eth0 inet static
        address 192.168.1.2
        network 192.168.1.0
        netmask 255.255.255.0
        gateway 192.168.1.1
        broadcast 192.168.1.255

using the right gateway etc for your network.

The other suggestion in the bug thread was to install dhclient, but that
would be difficult without a network connection unless it is on your
installation CD.

Bill Barry

On Nov 14, 2007, at 7:58 AM, Keith Lofstrom wrote:
>
> Apache/Web question:
> I have a few https web pages on my site with URLs like cj33wq.html .
> These drive CGI scripts that do stuff for clients, send me messages,
> etc.  They are not linked to (unless my clients do so - bad client!).

Thought about del.icio.us? Sometimes well intentioned people link/ 
bookmark "hidden" access points in public ways...

> Nothing falls apart if the bad guys find them and start frobbing on
> them;  it is just mildly inconvenient.  While most are password
> protected, some are not (like the page where they prove who they are
> then tell me what they want for a password).
> If I understand Apache and web service, there is no way that the bad
> guys can find these pages without an exhaustive search (which would
> be obvious from the logs) or by listening in on my client's traffic.

Brute force web hacks exist, FWIW.

Somebody already mentioned Apache's index generation....

So, another frequent vector to consider: mod_speling is not always  
your friend. A reasonable apache setup designed to correct for  
CJ33WQ.htm will *happily* find cj33wq.html.

Oh, and it never hurts to deny unfriendlies/bots, unless, of course,  
your denial gives more exploit/other information away.

See:
<http://www.whitehouse.gov/robots.txt>

For a few breathtaking pauses on the issue.

-Bop

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Nov 17 12:04:56 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sat, 17 Nov 2007 12:04:56 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Gutsy upgrade killed /dev/md3
Message-ID: <20071117120456.8f90de8a.johnxj@comcast.net>

I think I can fix it, but I need to borrow someone's brains to do so.

Yesterday I decided to upgrade my desktop from Feisty to Gutsy (amd64). Feisty was the original installation, done when I built the computer back in April. The machine has two 320 GB hard drives set up as RAID1 devices:

/dev/md0	4GB		swap
/dev/md1	50GB	/
/dev/md2 20GB	formatted ext3, unused
/dev/md3	230GB	formatted ext3, contained backups and some distro ISOs

The last two were mounted at /media/md2 and /media/md3 in fstab. Everything worked. Others here may remember a long thread when I built the computer because of problems creating the arrays. The problems disappeared after I returned the POS Abit motherboard to Fry's and bought an Asus instead. In other words, all my hair pulling was due to a buggy SATA-2 controller on the Abit board. The system ran flawlessly after replacing the motherboard.

After doing a dist-upgrade (using Update Manager GUI), upon restarting the computer, I got dumped to a root shell. The error message was that /dev/md3 did not exist due to a bad superblock. I could continue to boot with ctrl-d, whereupon I discovered that it did not mount either md2 or md3. I could manually mount md2, but md3 does not exist. It is odd that fstab lists both and there is nothing wrong with md2, yet it failed to mount it as well as md3. The relevant lines in fstab are:

#the following two lines added by me 4/30/2007
/dev/md2		/media/md2	ext3	defaults,errors=remount-ro	0	1
/dev/md3		/media/md3	ext3	defaults,errors=remount-ro	0	1

I found a bug report that appears relevant here:

https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/mdadm/+bug/155355

While the upgrade did not change /etc/fstab, it did make a new /etc/mdadm/mdadm.conf file. The old one said:

ARRAY /dev/md0 level=raid1 num-devices=2 UUID=<long-ass number here>
ARRAY /dev/md1 level=raid1 num-devices=2 UUID=<long-ass number here>
ARRAY /dev/md2 level=raid1 num-devices=2 UUID=<long-ass number here>
#ARRAY /dev/md3 level=raid1 num-devices=2 UUID=

The new one created by the upgrade says:

ARRAY /dev/md0 level=raid1 num-devices=2 UUID=<long-ass number here>
ARRAY /dev/md1 level=raid1 num-devices=2 UUID=<long-ass number here>
ARRAY /dev/md2 level=raid1 num-devices=2 UUID=<long-ass number here>
ARRAY /dev/md3 level=raid1 num-devices=/dev/sda4, /dev/sdb4

Now that is just weird. First, how did it work in the past when the line for md3 was commented out? And second, what possessed the upgrade script (I assume it was a script) to uncomment the md3 line, and then use the device list for the number of devices?

I attempted to use tune2fs to find the UUID number for md3, thinking that I could pop it in where it belongs in the new mdadm.conf file. However, tune2fs says the device does not exist.

I think the problem is exactly what the root shell error message said - that there is a bad superblock for md3. However, so far I have failed to resurrect it. I have man-paged mdadm, but can't figure out how to find the location of the backup superblock. Or does one use mdadm for that? I also tried e2fsck, e.g., "sudo e2fsck -b 8192" (changing the "8192" to 16384 and 32768, per man e2fsck), but neither works.

It's not the end of the world if I have to wipe out md3 and start over. It contained only backups and some distro ISOs that I can recreate and re-download. Nevertheless, if I can restore the superblock and that fixes it, then I can save a major annoyance.

Any suggestions welcome!

From nickaubert at gmail.com  Sun Nov 18 16:42:12 2007

	<55ef240d0711162235i636fecceq16048027c620ef1c@mail.gmail.com>
	<422ec1510711170742w271a6136l222ca8da290465d9@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <55ef240d0711181642g430aa2d9g8e98c2c8bbe626b5@mail.gmail.com>

You OpenSUSE laptop has a 192.168.x.x IP address, which is in the
range that most commodity home firewalls or routers should give out.
Since your Ubuntu machine doesn't have an IP address in the same
range, it's not getting one from the same place as your OpenSUSE
laptop (presumably it's getting it from whatever firewall or router
you plug your machines into to connect to the internet).

Can you confirm that your ethernet connection is set to DHCP?
Unfortunately, I don't know how to do that on Ubuntu.

-Nick

On Nov 18, 2007 9:49 PM, kurt braget <joehaircut at gmail.com> wrote:
> Yeah, I wish I knew how to do that. If anybody does, please let me know.
> Thanks!
>
>

First you could try a static ip to see if that fixes the problem. To
temporarily try a static ip use the following commands

sudo ifconfig eth0 192.168.1.25
sudo route add default gw 192.168.1.1

then test the connection by pinging yahoo.com
ping -c 1 216.109.112.135

If this returns something like

PING 216.109.112.135 (216.109.112.135) 56(84) bytes of data.
64 bytes from 216.109.112.135: icmp_seq=1 ttl=44 time=117 ms

Then it works and you can figure out how to make the changes permanent.

Bill Barry

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sun Nov 18 23:11:34 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Sun, 18 Nov 2007 23:11:34 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] How fast is my network, really?
Message-ID: <20071118231134.2e55ab79.johnxj@comcast.net>

My laptop and my desktop are connected over ethernet, all components of
which are cat6, and each computer has gigabit lan built in. Since Aaron
helped me get my backup partition on the desktop fixed at the Clinic
today, this evening I decided it was time to back up the laptop to the
desktop. By my calculation it ran at about 105 MB per minute. However,
I cannot figure out how fast "gigabit" is in terms of real bytes. I
noted that the hard drives on the desktop (which is SATA-2) came on and
off, and the laptop (which is probably SATA-1, and definitely slower)
was only flickering, not on constantly. Thus, I conclude that the
network was the bottleneck, not the hard disks. Or maybe the bottleneck
is dar, which is the tool I used (via Home User Backup). Both computers
run Gutsy x86_64.

Is 105 MB/minute a decent rate? What is typical in the real world for
this kind of equipment?

From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)

On Sun, Nov 18, 2007 at 11:11:34PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> My laptop and my desktop are connected over ethernet, all components of
> which are cat6, and each computer has gigabit lan built in. Since Aaron
> helped me get my backup partition on the desktop fixed at the Clinic
> today, this evening I decided it was time to back up the laptop to the
> desktop. By my calculation it ran at about 105 MB per minute. 

1GB = 125 MB = 119 MiB.  Speaking as someone who's just finished a
12-month contract testing network hardware at Intel, 105 MB per second
would be really good.  I can't guess why you'd see 1/60th of that,
unless you're actually running at 100Mbps, or even WiFi at 54 Mbps.


From krisa at subtend.net  Mon Nov 19 01:12:01 2007
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Mon, 19 Nov 2007 01:12:01 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] How fast is my network, really?
In-Reply-To: <20071118231134.2e55ab79.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20071118231134.2e55ab79.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <47415361.5000700@subtend.net>

John Jason Jordan wrote:
> desktop. By my calculation it ran at about 105 MB per minute. However,

105MB/m / 60 = 1.75MB/s

1.75MB/s * 8 = 14Mb/s

That's quite low for gig, even on commodity desktop hardware.  I backed up a
server the other night to my T61 over GigE, and was getting 110MB/s (6600MB/m).

Iperf works great for performance testing.
http://dast.nlanr.net/projects/iperf/

Thanks to both you and Aaron for the info. Evidently something is
seriously hanging things up. It seems to me that it must be either the
connection (wiring or components) or the software (dar - Home User
Backup). It should not be the hard disks, because even SATA-1 is
supposed to be good for 150 MB/second. And both computers definitely
have gigabit ethernet, although one could certainly question whether
the drivers are up to snuff. One of them is my T61 laptop.

I have a crossover cable, so I decided to do the backup over again this
morning with a direct connection to see if it is the wiring.
Unfortunately, as I took the cable out of its plastic wrap (bought it,
never used it before), I noted that it was only Cat5, not even Cat5e.
So I decided to test the dar software first. I have a folder on the
laptop that contains 8.9 GB of MP3 files (according to Nautilus - du
says it is 9371860, which I guess is MB). I used Nautilus and dragged
and dropped the whole thing onto the desktop. It took 7:38 (7.63
minutes), which I guess comes out to (using the du figure) 1.228290957
GB/min, or 20.471515 MB/sec. That's still about five times slower than
it should be. However, it is a vast improvement over the performance of
dar, so evidently dar is doing a lot of computing. I note that dar put
everything into one huge .dar file. The Home User Backup GUI front end
for it is not very helpful - you can't tell if it is compressing or
what it is doing. Then again, I don't know exactly what Nautilus does
when it copies a file. And when I did the copy this morning with
Nautilus, neither hard drive light was on constantly.

I looked at the website for lperf. It looked like it would take me a
while to figure out how to use it. I need something to test the wiring,
hardware and drivers independently of the backup/copy software. Is that
what lperf will do?

Is there a reason you need avahi?  In the past I have read no end of
complaints about how avahi (and zeroconf before it) screws with users'
abilities to connect to the Internet.  Personally, I avoid it like the
plague, but obviously someone somewhere has uses for it or it wouldn't
exist.  Maybe you need it for something.

If not, the first thing I would do is shutdown and disable the
avahi-daemon.  That alone might fix things.

Second thing I would do is disable IPv6, but that's just because it has
caused me huge problems with Debian and Ubuntu.  In other distros I've
tried (and on OS X), IPv6 wasn't a problem, so I wouldn't necessarily
take it as a given that because your openSUSE installation has IPv6
enabled, that couldn't be part of the problem you're having with Ubuntu.

>>
>> 1.75MB/s * 8 = 14Mb/s
>>

>> server the other night to my T61 over GigE, and was getting 110MB/s (6600MB/m).
>>
>> Iperf works great for performance testing.
>> http://dast.nlanr.net/projects/iperf/
> 
> Thanks to both you and Aaron for the info. Evidently something is
> seriously hanging things up. It seems to me that it must be either the
> connection (wiring or components) or the software (dar - Home User
> Backup). It should not be the hard disks, because even SATA-1 is
> supposed to be good for 150 MB/second. And both computers definitely
> have gigabit ethernet, although one could certainly question whether
> the drivers are up to snuff. One of them is my T61 laptop.

Just because the interface to the drive is 150 MB/s doesn't mean your 
drive can fill it.  For example, a recent model Seagate Momentus 
5400.4 drive has a SATA-3 interface, but it's sustained rate is 806 
Mb/s, or roughly 200 MB/s.  You may want to run the disk/filesystem 
performance tool 'bonnie' to get an idea of your disks' throughput.

galen


From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Nov 19 09:44:54 2007
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 19 Nov 2007 09:44:54 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] How fast is my network, really?
In-Reply-To: <20071118231134.2e55ab79.johnxj@comcast.net>
References: <20071118231134.2e55ab79.johnxj@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <20071119174454.GB20337@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Sun, Nov 18, 2007 at 11:11:34PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> My laptop and my desktop are connected over ethernet, all components of
> which are cat6, and each computer has gigabit lan built in. Since Aaron
> helped me get my backup partition on the desktop fixed at the Clinic
> today, this evening I decided it was time to back up the laptop to the
> desktop. By my calculation it ran at about 105 MB per minute. However,
> I cannot figure out how fast "gigabit" is in terms of real bytes. I
> noted that the hard drives on the desktop (which is SATA-2) came on and
> off, and the laptop (which is probably SATA-1, and definitely slower)
> was only flickering, not on constantly. Thus, I conclude that the
> network was the bottleneck, not the hard disks. Or maybe the bottleneck
> is dar, which is the tool I used (via Home User Backup). Both computers
> run Gutsy x86_64.
> 
> Is 105 MB/minute a decent rate? What is typical in the real world for
> this kind of equipment?

The disk lights don't say anything about usage factor - they are 
designed to stay on long enough so that you can see that something
is happening.  The actual sector writes are a lot faster than any
blink you can see.

You are almost certainly seeing disk speed and program speed rather
than gigabit ethernet speed limits.  See:

http://wiki.dirvish.org/index.cgi?SataDrives

... for some information on SATA-1 speeds.  You should expect more
than 30MB/sec for SATA-1 desktop hardware, less for laptop drives
(which are power limited) .  A best-of-breed backup program built 
around rsync will keep the disks busy because it juggles multiple
files at once, and will do so even with slow networks because it
transfers only the changes over the network.  Most backup programs
are not that sophisticated, and some dodder along with a  read-
think-write-think-verify-think process that can be very slow.

With dirvish (rsync based) and a 100mb network, I back up 7 
machines and 120GB in less than 3 hours.  That includes 2 machines
that are 1000+ miles away.  The resulting backup images are 
complete, directly readable, and mountable/bootable.  They are
hardlinked to other images so I can typically get 300+ complete
images on a 500GB hard drive.

There are tools that test disk speed, and tools that test networks.
I imagine your gigabit network is capable of 100MB/sec or so.  My
general rule of thumb is bit speed divided by 10, which can be a
little pessimistic or a lot optimistic.  Wifi is usually slower
because of increased latency.

Keith

Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

Try this:

   sudo mii-tool eth0

If there are any 10M or 100M devices on your switch, it is likely that
all connected devices will automatically slow down to match the slowest
device. There have been a few articles about this lately.

I had wondered why my colo network's back net has been so slow since I
plugged a 100T NIC into the switch, and now I know.

> while to figure out how to use it. I need something to test the wiring,
> hardware and drivers independently of the backup/copy software. Is that
> what lperf will do?
>
Iperf will do network testing for you.
On the server: iperf -b -u
On the client: iperf -c SERVER_IP_ADDR -b 1000Mb

Most computers/kernel will need to be tuned to use Gig-E to full
capacity.  NAPI enable drivers
are a must.

Carlos Konstanski <ckonstanski at pippiandcarlos.com> dijo:

> > I don't know anything about dar, and this might not be remotely true,
> > but if there is encryption going on, there might be CPU bottlenecks.
